           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <blockquote>
             The anonymous photo said to be of pink slime, which has come to describe beef cuts treated with ammonium hydroxide, has been floating around the Internet for years. The photo even has its own YouTube song (pink slime/pink goo/and you).
             <p>
              But the U.S. Department of Agriculture, which is buying 7 million pounds of the treated beef with the help of schools in the coming months, says the photo shows something else.
             </p>
             <p>
              What the photo actually shows is mechanically separated chicken, the kind of chicken used in chicken nuggets. As the blog Fooducate explains, an invention in the 1960s found that meat processors could get the last few scraps of chicken off the bird by scraping the bones of the meat. The method is called AMR, or Advanced Meat Recovery. And the above photo showing a pink and creamy paste is the result.
             </p>
            </blockquote>
            <a href="http://www.washingtonpost.com/blogs/blogpost/post/pink-slime-photo-isnt-what-it-appears-to-be/2012/03/09/gIQApJJd1R_blog.html">
             http://www.washingtonpost.com/...
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">
            <p class="sig">
             "I am not interested in picking up crumbs of compassion thrown from the table of someone who considers himself my master. I want the full menu of rights." (From "You Said a Mouthful" by Bishop Desmond Tutu - South African bishop &amp; activist, b.1931)
            </p>
            <p class="cb">
             by
             <a href="/user/uid:238869">
              FiredUpInCA
             </a>
             on
             <a href="/comments/1271430/52542623#c96">
              Mon Feb 03, 2014 at 11:36:57 AM PST
             </a>
            </p>
            <p class="cl">
             [
             <a href="#c71">
              Parent
             </a>
             ]
            </p>
           </div>
          </div>
         </li>
         <ul class="cm i1" id="k52542623">
          <li id="c98">
           <div class="cx" id="cx52543027">
            <h5>
             <a class="de">
             </a>
             <span class="cu">
              A food product, being manually dumped
             </span>
             <span class="crd ntb">
              (
              <a href="/comments/1271430/52543027#c98?mode=alone;showrate=1#c98">
               2+ / 0-
              </a>
              )
             </span>
            </h5>
            <div class="crs">
             <dl>
              <dt>
               Recommended by:
              </dt>
              <dd>
               <a href="/user/uid:330318">
                JerryNA
               </a>
               ,
               <a href="/user/uid:918758">
                thanatokephaloides
               </a>
              </dd>
             </dl>
            </div>
            <div class="ct">
             <p>
              into an unlined cardboard box, with stacks of those boxes sitting around at room temperature (see right side of image)?
             </p>
             <p>
              Also, as HuffPo notes on it's
              <a href="http://www.huffingtonpost.com/2010/10/04/mechanically-separated-meat-chicken-mcnugget-photo_n_749893.html?ref=fb&amp;src=sp&amp;comm_ref=false">
               article
              </a>
              :
             </p>
             <blockquote>
              Because the original source of the photo cannot be fully verified, we have removed it from this post
             </blockquote>
             The HuffPo article also has shots of actual "mechanically separated meat/chicken", which looks nothing like the substance in the photo. Note, especially, the consistency of the substance, which as you can see, especially from the boxes in the background, retains its shape. The goop in the videos linked in the HuffPo article turns into containers full of goop.
             <br/>
             Again, I don't know what the original photo is (and until or unless someone tracks down its source, no one does, which is a major minus to its credibility), but it looks nothing like what it purports to be.
            </div>

        <li id="c83">
         <div class="cx" id="cx52541904">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Markets themselves are amoral
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1271430/52541904#c83?mode=alone;showrate=1#c83">
             4+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:330318">
              JerryNA
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:255072">
              jan4insight
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:991156">
              todamo13
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:918758">
              thanatokephaloides
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            That's neither a good thing or a bad thing in and of itself, it simply means that markets have no built in morality. It's up to society therefore to impose morality on the market if they decide that's what they want. We already do this to a certain extent--you're not allowed to accept money to kill people, or sell other people for example.  It's really therefore a matter of degree--we've already accepted that regulating markets to fit societal morals is ok.
           </p>
           <p>
            This is something free marketers really don't understand. We impose a lot of restrictions on free markets. That's because we like it better that way. Without market restrictions, there really is no wrong way to make a buck, but I think even the most ardent libertarian would say that's wrong if they actually thought it through.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
           </p>

         <div class="cx" id="cx52544370">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Where's all the history buffs out there?
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1271430/52544370#c117?mode=alone;showrate=1#c117">
             3+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:332663">
              helpImdrowning
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:70396">
              AoT
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:918758">
              thanatokephaloides
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Love this diary, but it's missing the classical historical quote. I can't remember the name or date (my memory doesn't work that way), but it was from a law-educated Brit (I believe) from about a 100 years or so before the founding of the USA - he wrote books on law and philosophy which were studied by our country's founding fathers. And the statement goes
            <strong>
             something
            </strong>
            like this:
           </p>
           <p>
            <em>
             Corporations are not moral entities. They have no soul or conscience to guide them and no body to punish. They exist for the sole purpose of making profit.
            </em>
           </p>
           <p>
            Sorry I don't remember the entire quote, and probably got some of what I remember wrong. But the intent left a strong impact on my way of thinking about economics vs social justice. So maybe one of our history buffs can dig up the fact details (who, when, exact quote)? It's worth a mention.
           </p>
           <p>
            And then maybe somebody can speak to the history and founding of OSHA, and the history and formation of workers' unions in this country? They just don't teach much of this stuff in school, but it's important history.
           </p>
           <p>
            Unfortunately,
            <strong>
             I
            </strong>
            didn't study my history in school - back then it was mostly about wars and names and dates, which I don't remember well and which didn't interest me. But as an adult I have a lot of interest in people's "stories", and this type of history draws me in. I also care about worker safety, and am subscribed to the FDA Recall website and the CPSC Recall website. If you ever needed justification for why we need government regulation of business, just take a stroll through one of these websites.
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             <a href="http://www.cpsc.gov/en/Recalls/">
              http://www.cpsc.gov/...
             </a>
            </strong>
           </p>
           <p>
            <strong>
             <a href="http://www.fda.gov/Safety/Recalls/default.htm">
              http://www.fda.gov/...
             </a>
            </strong>
            <br/>
           </p>

        <li id="c127">
         <div class="cx" id="cx52547261">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            I'm in CT right now
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1271430/52547261#c127?mode=alone;showrate=1#c127">
             1+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:918758">
              thanatokephaloides
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            It snowed all day. As the light was fading in the evening, I was driving on plowed streets as dozens of people shoveled their walks and the stretch of sidewalk in front of their own homes. Everyone was going about their business, despite 5" of snow having fallen throughout the day.
           </p>
           <p>
            And I reflected how the city of Atlanta got reduced to disaster and chaos by a mere 2" of snow, something that New Englanders would barely have noticed. Perhaps it's just that Yankees have cultural traditions of thinking in terms of their town, their community. No one much has a problem with the expenditures necessary to have dump trucks with sand and/or salt, and plows on front driving around all day - at taxpayer expense! to take care of it.
           </p>
           <p>
            Talking to a friend in the South, I got the wry observation back: "Private markets are the solution." The other thing that makes me a life-long northerner, no matter where I live? I prefer not to think of "Bless your heart" as an insult.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            Mark Twain: It ain't what you don't know that gets you into trouble. It's what you know for sure that just ain't so.
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:63399">
             Land of Enchantment
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1271430/52547261#c127">
             Mon Feb 03, 2014 at 08:22:14 PM PST
            </a>
           </p>
           <p class="cl">
           </p>
          </div>
         </div>
        </li>
        <ul class="cm i1" id="k52547261">
         <li id="c140">
          <div class="cx" id="cx52549652">
           <h5>
            <a class="de">
            </a>
            <span class="cu">
             I used to think that way, too ....
            </span>
            <span class="crd ntb">
             (
             <a href="/comments/1271430/52549652#c140?mode=alone;showrate=1#c140">
              1+ / 0-
             </a>
             )
            </span>
           </h5>
           <div class="crs">
            <dl>
             <dt>
              Recommended by:
             </dt>
             <dd>
              <a href="/user/uid:237632">
               Charles Hall
              </a>
             </dd>
            </dl>
           </div>
           <div class="ct">
            <p>
             I'm what the south calls a "D**n Yankee". I grew up in Michigan, lived in upstate NY for a while, then moved to NC and have been here 27 years.
            </p>
            <p>
             When I first move south, I would drive in the snow here. But I quickly discovered that snow in the south is different than snow in the north. (As are the drivers.)
            </p>
            <p>
             The winter temps when it snows are usually just a little below freezing, or a little above freezing, in the daytime. The frost line is only 2" deep, so pavement may be warmer than freezing at first. And the sun also heats the dark asphalt paving in the daytime. So when the snow first falls, it melts, leaving a wet surface. Then as more snow falls, it covers the wet surface and starts to stick.
            </p>
            <p>
             People with tread on their tires (you'd be surprised how many southerners run their tires down to bald) can then drive on the snow for a while. As more cars drive on it, it packs down and some of it melts. Then as darkness falls, it gets colder, and all the melted snow refreezes.
            </p>
            <p>
             You now have slick as snot ice covering the roads, and since studded snow tires are not allowed in the south, you have a mess. People start sliding off the roads. As their cars get stuck, or smacked up, people abandon their cars where they stopped, and walk away. Now you not only have icy roads, but an obstacle course to navigate as well.
            </p>
            <p>
             So now when it snows, I just stay home until the snow has melted off and the roads are clear.
            </p>
            <p>
             I shovel my porch steps and walkway, but my driveway is gravel so I don't shovel that. And there are no sidewalks in my subdivision (or most subdivisions outside larger city limits or downtown areas).
            </p>
            <p>
             Southerners do have a sense of community. It just comes out in different ways. Neighbors are quick to check in on each other after a major storm, or after a major health event. People take food to their neighbors when a family member is ill or injured. Etc.
            </p>
            <p>
             And while I quickly learned that "Bless your heart" can mean anything, depending on the circumstances and tone of voice, I have found that it can be diffused by pretending it's always meant literally (nicely), and responding in a gracious manner and with a smile. (Nothing takes the fun out of an insult quicker than refusing to be insulted.)
            </p>
           </div>
           <div class="cf">
            <p class="sig">
            </p>
            <p class="cb">
             by
             <a href="/user/uid:991235">
              bethann
             </a>
             on
             <a href="/comments/1271430/52549652#c140">
              Tue Feb 04, 2014 at 07:42:58 AM PST
             </a>
            </p>
            <p class="cl">
             [
             <a href="#c127">
              Parent
             </a>
             ]
            </p>
           </div>
          </div>
         </li>
        </ul>
        <li id="c130">
         <div class="cx" id="cx52547756">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Two quotes I've read: "Avarice is a disease"
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1271430/52547756#c130?mode=alone;showrate=1#c130">
             2+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="crs">
           <dl>
            <dt>
             Recommended by:
            </dt>
            <dd>
             <a href="/user/uid:918758">
              thanatokephaloides
             </a>
             ,
             <a href="/user/uid:991235">
              bethann
             </a>
            </dd>
           </dl>
          </div>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            and "Madness is badness of spirit, when one seeks profit from all sources - attributed to Aristotle
           </p>
           <p>
            The first one strikes me as a clear-headed way to put this greedhead business. It's proper name is "Avarice" the inordinate desire for wealth. Classed as one of the seven major soul-killing mistakes ('mortal sin') a body can make in this life.
           </p>
           <p>

        <li id="c144">
         <div class="cx" id="cx52549956">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Whenever I make the argument
           </span>

            I'm glad to see this as I'm working on messaging,
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1271430/52565262#c163?mode=alone;showrate=1#c163">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            but I'd like to have seen some images of US (and global) farmers, in that they've mostly been put out of business by farm bill deregulation, (covered up with the fake issue of subsidies).
           </p>
           <p>
            First they came for the black farmers, then they added the chicken farmers, then they went out to eliminate 1/3 of us, then they expanded it. By the 90s they went after the hog farmers (4 corporations own most US hogs), and for 20 years it's been the dairy farmers. These have been the canaries in the mines, and most categories are dying, with most members of the categories already out of business. Livestock farming is much worse than the low value crop farming, which is what farmers are increasingly reduced to.
           </p>
           <p>
            This is all mostly unknown by progressives, who think that farmers benefit, increasingly, from the farm bill. In fact, the latest farm bill is about the 8th general level of compromise downward. Not far below is Hooverism.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
            "We're trying to warn this nation of a tidal wave ..., and it's coming your way, whether you want to know it or not...!"  family farm woman, Donahue Show, 1985

          <div class="cf">

           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:102736">

        <li id="c172">
         <div class="cx" id="cx52567748">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            And there's no end in sight...
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1271430/52567748#c172?mode=alone;showrate=1#c172">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            Whatever "economic growth and recovery" has been accomplished since President Obama first took office on January 20, 2009, it hasn't helped anybody but Corporate America and the richest 1%. For the millions of average Americans, including the middle class, the working poor, unemployed folks, senior citizens, college students and military veterans, this economic BOOM has been more of a BUST.
           </p>
           <p>
            The gradually decreasing unemployment rate is touted as such a good statistic for measuring the health of the economy, but in reality, it's a joke. Many of the people who HAVE been able to find employment are not working full-time (40 hours per week), and thus they don't qualify for company benefits such as vacations, sick pay or health insurance. Some are earning half the salary they made before losing their jobs, and some are under-employed for the type of work they were trained and/or educated to perform. Of course, companies know that most unemployed folks are willing to do just about ANYTHING to earn money to support their families, and so the businesses are taking advantage of this fact by hiring desperate people, working them like Hebrew slaves, and paying them peanuts for their services.
           </p>
           <p>
            Most companies now want a non-union work environment, and they've either curtailed the power of the unions or shut them down outright. It doesn't help matters when we have Republican-led state legislatures who are killing the public workers' unions, such as has been done in Wisconsin. Corporations constantly piss and moan about the unions and rail against government regulations, saying that they kill business and profits. Well, if companies could be trusted to do the right thing by providing fair wages and fringe benefits, ensuring a safe and healthy workplace, protecting the environmment, and being a good neighbor to the surrounding community, then there wouldn't be any need for those pesky unions and regulations.
           </p>
           <p>
            I read an article earlier this week on Yahoo! Finance that said even the business community has now recognized and voiced the opinion that the middle class in is shrinking. Of course, Corporate America is planning accordingly, doing away with many of the stores where most average Americans shop, the products they buy, and the services they use. Instead, some companies have revised their marketing strategy to increase targeting to the wealthiest 1%, where consumption is on the rise, and decrease marketing to the middle class, where it's declining.
           </p>
           <p>
            This "economic growth and recovery" Corporate America and the fat cats are enjoying is built on a house of cards. One day in the future, probably sooner rather than later, it's all going to come tumbling down again. I'd like to say, "And what will they do THEN?" But of course, the people and corporations at the top will come out smelling like a rose. AGAIN. Back when the stock market crashed in 1929, bringing on unemployment, poverty and despair for regular folks, the wealthy also suffered, blowing their brains out or jumping out of high-rise buildings. Did we see such suicidal tendencies amongst the fat cats back in 2008? Of course not. Did we see anybody go to jail for their financial shenanigans? Nope.These rich motherfuckers have learned to hedge their bets, limiting their own culpability in the debacle and ensuring their own financial well-being, while the rest of us poor slobs suffer. This is economics and capitalism on crack.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">
           </p>
           <p class="cb">
            by
            <a href="/user/uid:317463">
             Tigger Too
            </a>
            on
            <a href="/comments/1271430/52567748#c172">
             Thu Feb 06, 2014 at 08:07:40 AM PST
            </a>
           </p>
           <p class="cl">
           </p>
          </div>

        <li id="c178">
         <div class="cx" id="cx52575087">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            supply and demand
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1271430/52575087#c178?mode=alone;showrate=1#c178">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            When the price of gold gets too low to make them profitable, gold mines close until the price goes back up again. When the price of labor gets too low for laborers to live on, they still sell their product on the market; otherwise they will starve. That's why the market for labor is different than the market for gold. Yet, capitalism is set up so that labor is always too plentiful to demand prosperous wages for its contribution to the common prosperity.
           </p>
           <p>
            One example: One of the criteria the Fed. Reserve uses to slow down the economy is the unemployment rate. When "too few" folks are unemployed and scrambling for a living, the economy is deliberately slowed down so that labor never gets too pricey. Another: In the 14th century, the plague so decimated the population that the merchants and craftsmen complained to the King of England that workers were actually bargaining for wages!! Imagine that. If one blacksmith in town paid too little, a skilled workman could walk over to the competition and ask for a better deal, knowing that the merchant might be desperate enough to part with a few guineas more to supply his labor needs. Naturally, the King froze wages, or at least tried to. But of course, he didn't freeze prices too. The 1300s were a time of lower class restlessness in Germany and England. There was even a proto-Reformation in England that had to be killed off with the noose and the auto de fe. I wonder why?
           </p>
           <p>
            And most recently: Once the great bugaboo Communism ceased being a threat, capital freed itself to exploit cheap labor overseas, driving down incomes in America. We see the results today. I needn't belabor the point. But suffice it to say we see capital opposing unemployment insurance, social security and medicare/medicaid - 'ya hold on to that desperate underclass to cow the ones with jobs. Health insurance: Might people get unstuck from sucky jobs they desperately cling to for the benefits they desperately need? Woo, scary, that one.
           </p>
          </div>
          <div class="cf">
           <p class="sig">

        <li id="c185">
         <div class="cx" id="cx56439656">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            Amoral capitalism
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1271430/56439656#c185?mode=alone;showrate=1#c185">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            One of the insidious evils of capitalism is that it is amoral. Practitioners excuse themselves by saying they are just making money providing a good or service without taking in consideration the long term or indirect effects of their actions. All kinds of perverse incentives can be justified in the pursuit of profit. For instance, a stock broker can make more money by using inside information. Clearly wrong and illegal. Without regulation it just becomes too easy to cheat. Compounding this problem is that when others find this out they will be incentivized to do the same thing to compete, otherwise they will be out of business. This is why regulation is necessary and why the idea of self regulation is bogus.
           </p>
          </div>

        <li id="c200">
         <div class="cx" id="cx56447747">
          <h5>
           <a class="de">
           </a>
           <span class="cu">
            The self-regulating market
           </span>
           <span class="crd ntb">
            (
            <a href="/comments/1271430/56447747#c200?mode=alone;showrate=1#c200">
             0+ / 0-
            </a>
            )
           </span>
          </h5>
          <div class="ct">
           <p>
            You said it yourself, Ak: "...
            <strong>
             people
            </strong>
            fought against child labor and slavery" -- not governments. Governments didn't stir a finger against these abuses until the
            <strong>
             people
            </strong>
            complained hard and long enough. In fact, governments often stood against the reforms, as in the case of labor unions, until overwhelmed by the numbers of the
            <strong>
             people
            </strong>
            demanding change.
           </p>
           <p>
            Too many bosses -- and lefty reformers -- forget that the public, the citizens, the working class, the victims, are part of the market-regulating system too. This is especially true today, with our modern communications that can reveal a scandal almost overnight. It's possible to organize a strike, a boycott, or more (think Molly Maguires and wooden shoes), very quickly and very effectively. It doesn't take government regulations to curb the excesses of bosses; it takes an informed and aware citizenry. Note how fast Firestone went under when news of its exploding tires spread.
           </p>
           <p>
            If anything, govt. regulations and bureaucracies are a hindrance to the process, because they lull the people into a "let Sam do it" complacency, instead of that Eternal Vigilance that's the price of liberty, safety, and everything else. It doesn't help that the govt. regulatory bureaucracies can readily be misled or suborned into actually protecting the bosses from the vengeance of their victims.
           </p>
           <p>
            Perhaps the worst thing you could do to these bosses who whine about govt. regulations would be to give them what they ask for; remove
            <strong>
             all
            </strong>
            regulations -- not only on businesses, but on labor unions, consumers' unions, whistleblowers, media, and all. Leave the bosses with no shield between themselves and the people at large, and believe me, they'd learn to walk very soft, very fast.
           </p>
           <p>
            Oh, and remove all gun-control laws too. History Hath Shewn that Jay Gould (IIRC) was wrong when he boasted that he could "hire half the working class to kill the other half", but Sam Colt was right when he called his invention an "equalizer". If you doubt that, study the history of the labor movement -- particularly in the mining industry. Note too that, despite 80 years' concerted effort by both bosses and govt., the IWW is still alive -- and currently organizing fast-food workers and computer programmers. Bon appetit.
           </p>
          </div>

           <p>

		DailyKos.Metrics.init();
  </script>
  <script type="text/javascript">
   if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/story/)) {
			  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Story', {  url: "http://www.dailykos.com/story/2014/02/03/1271430/-25-Images-of-Markets-Regulating-Themselves", 
			                                            blog: "akadjian",
			                                            author: $('.author').children('a').first().html(),
			                                            title: $('#titleHref').html()
			                                          });
			} else if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/comment/)) {
			  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Comment');
			}
  </script>
  <script type="text/javascript">
   if($.cookie('meter_user') !== 'false') {
		if ($('#ePostButton').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('#ePostButton').click(function() { 
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Posted Comment', { 
		      repliedTo: $(this).parents('#eP').prevAll('.cb').children('a').first().html() 
		    });
		  });
		}
		
		if (!!document.location.pathname.match('activate') &amp;&amp; $('h1:contains("Welcome to Daily Kos!")').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Signed Up');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname === '/') {
		  if (document.location.hostname.match(/^www|^dailykos/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Front Page');
		  } else {
		    var subdomain = document.location.hostname.split('.')[0];
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Vertical', { name: subdomain });
		  }
		}
		
		if ($('.ratinate').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.ratinate').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Comment') });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-addRating.type-story').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-addRating.type-story').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Recommended Story') });
		}
		
		if ($("form[action$='publishHow']").length &gt; 0) {
		  $(document).ready(function() {
			  $('.type-story.do-publishHow').click(function() { DailyKos.Metrics.track('Published Story') });
			 });
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/user/) &amp;&amp; $('li.here').length &gt; 0) {
		  if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Stream/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Stream', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:first li.here').children('a').html().match(/Profile/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Profile', { name: $('.name').children('a').html() });
		  } else if ($('.pageMenu:last li.here').children('a').html().match(/My Recent Replies/)) {
		    DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Replies');
		  }
		} 
		
		if ($('#search-tabbed').length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Searched');
		}
		
		if ($('#group-tables').length &gt; 0) {
			DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Groups List');
		}
		
		if (document.location.pathname.match(/^\/groups/) &amp;&amp; $("#group_info").length &gt; 0) {
		  DailyKos.Metrics.track('Viewed Group', { name: $('.name').children('a').text() });
		}
		
		if ($('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').length &gt; 0) {
		  $('.do-hotlist.type-user, .do-unHotlist.type-user').click(function() {
		    var nickName;
		    nickName = ($(this).prev('a').html() || $('.name a').html())
		    if ($(this).hasClass('do-hotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Followed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    } else if ($(this).hasClass('do-unHotlist')) {
		      DailyKos.Metrics.track('Unfollowed User', { nickname: nickName }) 
		    }    
		  });
		}
	}
  </script>
  <!-- end mixpanel -->
 </body>
</html>
